Understanding Trial (Ibtilaa) in the Quran and Its Educational Implications
Author defines the concept of trial, its forms, purpose, and educational objective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Quranic concept of trial (ibtilaa) and derive its educational implications using Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Trial includes both good and evil; its purpose is to purify and distinguish believers.

In this lesson, we explore the Quranic concept of trial (ibtilaa) as presented by the author. The passage defines trial's forms, location, and dual nature, emphasizing that both good and evil are tests from Allah. The ultimate purpose is discrimination and purification of believers. The author then extracts an educational objective: to cultivate patient, persevering learners who understand trial and can fulfill their role despite obstacles. By the end, you will be able to articulate these elements and apply them to Islamic educational thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the forms of trial
Find in the passage the specific forms of trial mentioned: wealth, oneself, and harm from others. Note the supporting Quranic verse (3:186).
Trial is not monolithic; it manifests in multiple aspects of life.
2
Locate the field of trial
The passage states that all trials occur on earth (18:7). Understand that earth is the arena where tests unfold.
This emphasizes the worldly nature of trial.
3
Recognize the dual nature of trial
The author highlights that trial includes both good and evil (21:35). Identify that trial is not limited to hardship; prosperity is also a test.
This counters the misconception that trial is only punishment.
4
Determine the purpose of trial
Extract the purpose from Quran 3:141-142: discrimination and purification of believers, and distinguishing levels of patience.
The ultimate goal is to separate the sincere from the hypocritical and to refine faith.
5
Derive the educational objective
Based on the purpose, the author concludes that education should produce patient, persevering learners who are aware of the sunnah of trial and can continue their role despite obstacles. Also note the method: learning by event.
This links Quranic theology to pedagogical practice.

الباب الثاني: الفصل الثاني- ميدان المعرفة - الغيب والشهادة
والمظهر الابتلاء صور عدة كالابتلاء في المال والنفس والتعرض لصنوف الأذى من جهات شتى:
لَتُبْلَوُنَّ فِي أَمْوَالِكُمْ وَأَنْفُسِكُمْ وَلَتَسْمَعُنَّ مِنَ الَّذِينَ أُوتُوا الْكِتابَ مِنْ قَبْلِكُمْ وَمِنَ الَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا أَذَى كَثِيراً وَإِنْ تَصْبِرُوا وَتَتَّقُوا فَإِنَّ ذَلِكَ مِنْ عَزْمِ الأمور ) [آل عمران: ١٨٦/٣].
وجميع صور الابتلاء تتم في ميدان الابتلاء وهو الأرض:
إنا جَعَلْنَا مَا عَلَى الْأَرْضِ زِينَةً لَهَا لِنَبْلُوَهُمْ أَيُّهُمْ أَحْسَنُ عَمَلاً﴾ [الكهف: ۷/۱۸]. ويلاحظ أن الابتلاء لا يلزم مظهراً واحداً هو الشر فحسب، بل إن ثمة مظهراً آخر له هو الخير، بما يمثل قسيماً للشر، ولذلك جاء التعبير عنهما في سياق واحد: ﴿وَنَبْلُوكُمْ بِالشَّرِّ وَالْخَيْرِ فِتْنَةٌ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٣٥/٢١].
أما هدف الابتلاء فالتمييز والتمحيص للمؤمنين عمن سواهم، كما يميز المؤمنين في مستويات الصبر على الابتلاء:
وَلِيُمَحِّصَ اللَّهُ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَيَمْحَقَ الْكَافِرِينَ، أَمْ حَسِبْتُمْ أَنْ تَدْخُلُوا الْجَنَّةَ وَلَمّا يَعْلَمِ اللَّهُ الَّذِينَ جَاهَدُوا مِنْكُمْ وَيَعْلَمَ الصابرين ﴾ [آل عمرن : ١٤١/٣-١٤٢]. لذلك كله يجب أن تشمل أهداف المنهج هدف إخراج المتعلم الصابر المثابر المدرك لسنة الابتلاء، كي يقوم بأداء دوره في مجتمعه رغم العوائق المختلفة، ولكنها لن تثنيه عن المضي في طريق الاستخلاف والبناء. كما تشمل سنة الابتلاء: أسلوب التعلم بالحَدَث من خلال المواقف المتعددة، والصور المتنوعة لمظهر الابتلاء.
Chapter Two: Second Section - The Field of Knowledge - The Unseen and the Witnessed. The manifestation of trial has several forms, such as trial in wealth and in oneself, and exposure to various kinds of harm from different directions: 'You will surely be tested in your possessions and in yourselves, and you will surely hear from those who were given the Scripture before you and from those who associate others with Allah much abuse. But if you are patient and fear Allah, indeed that is of the matters of determination.' [Al Imran 3:186]. And all forms of trial take place in the field of trial, which is the earth: 'Indeed, We have made that which is on the earth adornment for it that We may test them [as to] which of them is best in deed.' [Al-Kahf 18:7]. And it is noted that trial does not necessarily have only one manifestation, which is evil; rather, there is another manifestation of it, which is good, representing a counterpart to evil, and therefore they are expressed in a single context: 'And We test you with evil and with good as trial.' [Al-Anbiya 21:35]. As for the purpose of trial, it is discrimination and purification of the believers from others, and it distinguishes the believers in levels of patience upon trial: 'And that Allah may purify the believers and destroy the disbelievers. Or do you think that you will enter Paradise while Allah has not yet made evident those of you who fight in His cause and made evident those who are patient?' [Al Imran 3:141-142]. Therefore, the objectives of the curriculum must include the objective of producing a patient, persevering learner who is aware of the sunnah of trial, so that he may perform his role in his society despite various obstacles, yet they will not deter him from proceeding on the path of succession and construction. The sunnah of trial also includes: the method of learning by event through multiple situations and diverse forms of the manifestation of trial.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of trial (ibtilaa) in the Quran and its educational implications. The author begins by stating that trial has multiple forms, such as trial in wealth and in oneself, and exposure to harm from others, citing Quran 3:186. Then he explains that all trials occur on earth, referencing Quran 18:7. A key point is that trial is not limited to evil but includes good as well, as both are a test from Allah, supported by Quran 21:35. The purpose of trial is discrimination and purification of believers, as shown in Quran 3:141-142. Finally, the author derives an educational objective: to produce a patient, persevering learner who understands the sunnah of trial and can fulfill his role despite obstacles. The passage also mentions that learning by event is part of the sunnah of trial. The commentary follows the author's order: forms, location, dual nature, purpose, and educational goal.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic concept of trial includes both good and evil, occurs on earth, aims to purify believers, and informs an educational objective of producing patient, persevering learners who learn through events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Islamic vs Philosophical Sources of Knowledge
Epistemological distinction between Islamic and philosophical views on sources and methods of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Islamic conception of sources of knowledge from the philosophical one, citing the divine origin and the role of human instruments as empowered by God.

Core Idea
In Islam, the source of knowledge is divine; methods are God-given tools.

The passage introduces a key epistemological distinction: what is the origin of knowledge, and what are the means of acquiring it? While philosophy often conflates sources and methods, the Islamic view separates them, placing the ultimate source in God. This has profound implications for how we understand reason, senses, and revelation in Islamic education.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms 'sources of knowledge' (masādir al-ma‘rifa) and 'methods of knowledge' (ṭarā’iq al-ma‘rifa) in the Arabic. Notice how the author distinguishes them: sources are origins, methods are means.
The text states that some scholars use them interchangeably, but the Islamic view differentiates.
2
Understand the Islamic claim about divine origin
Find the statement that the origin of knowledge in Islam is divine (rabbānī). The Qur’anic verse (96:5) is cited: 'Taught man what he did not know.' This establishes God as the source.
This is contrasted with philosophy, which does not clarify man's relationship with the Creator.
3
Analyze the role of human instruments
Examine the phrase that even our use of reason and senses is only by God's enabling (bi-iqdār Allāh). This means tools are empowered, not independent sources.
In the philosophical view, the tools themselves are considered sources.
4
Recognize al-Ghazali's integration of reason
The passage quotes al-Ghazali calling reason 'the spring of knowledge, its source and foundation.' This shows that reason is valued but within the framework of divine empowerment.
Al-Ghazali's critique of philosophers did not reject reason; he placed it under divine authority.
5
Apply the distinction to Islamic education
Consider why this distinction matters: if the source is divine, then all knowledge-seeking must be grounded in tawḥīd, and methods like reason and observation are gifts from God.
This shapes the Islamic educational approach to integrating revealed and acquired knowledge.

يُشكّل موضوع المصادر والطرائق أهمية خاصة بالنسبة إلى التربية الإسلامية، لأنه يتصل ببناء فكرة الأمة، وتوجهها الحضاري من أجل إعادة بنائها، أفرادا وجماعات، لانطلاقها من الجذور الأصلية والاتجاهات والقيم التي كان لها أكبر الأثر في تاريخنا (۱).
ومن هنا فإن التربية الإسلامية تقف موقفاً موضوعياً علمياً في نظرتها لأنواع المعرفة، إذ هي تأخذ في الاعتبار كلّ الآراء والأنواع التي تعود على الإنسان بمزيد من المعرفة (٢).
ولكن الدارسين لهذا الموضوع ليسوا على كلمة سواء، فيما يتصل بتصورهم لهذه المصادر والطرائق أكان ذلك من حيث الاتفاق على إطلاق هذا المصطلح على المصادر والطرائق معًا، أو التمييز بين كلّ منهما والأخرى، على معنى أنهما اسمان لمسمى واحد ، أو أن المصادر شيء، والطرائق شيء مختلف تماماً.
وإذا شئنا المضي في معرفة الأمر لدى بعض الذين تناولوه فسنجد ما يلي:

Introduction. The topic of sources and methods is of particular importance for Islamic education, because it is connected to building the nation's idea and its civilizational orientation in order to rebuild it, individuals and communities, to launch from the original roots and directions and values that had the greatest impact on our history (1). Hence, Islamic education takes an objective scientific stance in its view of types of knowledge, as it takes into account all opinions and types that bring more knowledge to man (2). But scholars on this subject are not in agreement regarding their conception of these sources and methods, whether in terms of agreeing to apply this term to sources and methods together, or distinguishing between each of them, meaning that they are two names for one thing, or that sources are something and methods are something completely different. If we wish to proceed in knowing the matter among some who have addressed it, we will find the following: Chapter Three - Introduction. First: Some researchers' conception of the term. One researcher indicates saying: Philosophy uses (sources of knowledge) and means by it the methods or means of knowledge, but the term sources of knowledge in the philosophical concept is not what is meant by its methods according to our precise conception, because the source of a thing is its origin, and the origin of knowledge with us is divine: 'Taught man what he did not know' [Al-Alaq 96:5]. Even our use of the tools and means of knowledge, such as reason and sense, is only by God's enabling - Almighty - and empowering these means to perform their cognitive role. While the relationship of man to his Creator is not clear in the philosophical concept, hence their methods of knowledge are their sources and origins together (1). The truth is that this is not a purely philosophical usage, for here is Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali (d. 505 AH) who exposed the incoherence of the divine (metaphysical) philosophers in a way unprecedented, and I do not think anyone after him will approach his mastery of philosophical studies, as attested by his book 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers' and 'The Deliverer from Error'. And we find that he considers reason the spring of knowledge, its source and foundation, saying: 'Reason is the spring of knowledge, its source and foundation, and knowledge flows from it like the fruit from the tree, the light from the sun, and the sight from the eye' (2). That, and one researcher explains the source as meaning the spring, origin, and passage from which we reach this knowledge (3). Accordingly, some researchers proceeded in their use of sources in the context of talking about methods and vice versa, as you notice from one of them when he presented the methodology of Ibn
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the concept of sources and methods of knowledge in Islamic education. It begins by asserting the importance of the topic for rebuilding the Islamic nation from its original roots and values. The author then notes that Islamic education takes a scientific and objective stance, considering all types of knowledge that benefit man. However, scholars disagree on whether the terms 'sources' and 'methods' refer to the same thing or are distinct. The passage then presents the view of one researcher who distinguishes between the Islamic and philosophical concepts. According to this researcher, philosophy uses 'sources of knowledge' to mean methods, but in the Islamic view, the source is the origin of a thing, and the ultimate origin is God, as indicated by the Quranic verse 'Taught man what he did not know'. Human tools like reason and senses are only effective by God's empowerment, whereas philosophy does not clarify man's relationship with the Creator. The author then clarifies that this does not mean Islamic thought rejects reason, citing Imam al-Ghazali, who considered reason to be the spring, source, and foundation of knowledge, comparing it to fruit from a tree, light from the sun, and sight from the eye. Finally, the passage notes that some researchers use 'sources' and 'methods' interchangeably, illustrating the terminological fluidity in the field.

A) In Islam, reason is the sole source of knowledge.
B) In Islam, the origin of knowledge is divine, and human instruments are empowered by God.
C) In Islam, sources and methods are always identical.

Key Takeaway
In Islam, the ultimate source of knowledge is God, while reason and senses are tools empowered by Him—a distinction that shapes Islamic epistemology and education.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Distinguishing Sources and Means of Knowledge in Islamic Epistemology
Author clarifies the distinction between sources and means of knowledge, critiquing researcher confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sources (masadir) and means (tara'iq) of knowledge as defined in the passage and apply this distinction to Islamic epistemological concepts.

Core Idea
Sources are foundations; means are tools—confusing them leads to intellectual harm.

In Islamic epistemology, terms like 'source' and 'means' are often used interchangeably, but the author insists on a precise distinction. By examining the passage, you will learn why this distinction matters and how it prevents conceptual confusion in fields like Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam. We will explore the definitions, the debate about the intellect, and the Quranic basis for revelation as both source and tool.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key terms
Locate in the Arabic where the author defines 'sources' (المصادر) and 'means' (الطرائق). Note that the author presents them as distinct despite some researchers using them synonymously.
The terms appear in the second paragraph: 'الأصول التي يستقي منها الباحث المسلم معرفته' (foundations from which the Muslim researcher derives knowledge) and 'الأدوات التي يتعامل بها مع الظواهر المختلفة' (tools for dealing with various phenomena).
2
Extract the author's definitions
From the author's resolution, write down the clear definitions: sources are the origins of knowledge (revelation, senses, intellect); means are the methods of engaging with phenomena (the same three plus secondary sources as tools? Actually, secondary sources are not means). Understand that secondary sources (intuition, inspiration, true dreams) are sources but not means due to their subjective nature.
The passage says: 'أما الحدس والإلهام والرؤيا الصادقة فمصادر ثانوية لا تتجاوز ذلك إلى الطرائق'.
3
Analyze the debate about the intellect
The passage presents two opposing views: one argues intellect is both source and means; another denies it as a source because the Quran only uses 'aql as a verb. Find the author's resolution: the intellect carries innate laws (hence a source) but functions primarily as a tool.
The author concludes: 'العقل يحمل في ثناياه قوانين فطرية أصيلة مما تعده مصدراً بلا تجاوز، وإن كان أداة في غالب وظائفه'.
4
Identify the Quranic support
The author cites verses that show revelation as both source and instrument (e.g., Q 21:45, 42:52). Understand how these verses illustrate the dual role of revelation as a source (the content) and a means (the 'light' that guides).
Verse 42:52: 'وَمَا كُنْتَ تَدْرِي مَا الْكِتَابُ وَلَا الْإِيمَانُ وَلَٰكِنْ جَعَلْنَاهُ نُورًا نَهْدِي بِهِ مَنْ نَشَاءُ'—revelation is both the message and the guide.
5
Recognize the warning against confusing sources with fields
The final part criticizes researchers who mistake 'fields' (like anfus and afaq) for sources, or who attribute to the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya that reason is a 'field' of knowledge. This reinforces the need for precise terminology.
This step helps you avoid common errors in Islamic studies.

الباب الثالث - مقدمة

تيمية في النظام المعرفي، فوصفه بالتكامل بين مصادر المعرفة الوحي والعقل والحواس. وفي موطن لاحق عبر بالمصادر والوسائل معاً بوصفهما اسمين لمسمى واحد فقال:

((إن إسقاط أي مصدر أو وسيلة من وسائل المعرفة (الوحي أو العقل أو الحس أو جعله بديلاً أو مقابلاً لغيره سوف يلحق بالمعرفة كثيراً من الإصابات الفكرية. ولكن ثمة من يرى فرقًا بين المصطلحين، فلا يمكن إجمال المصادر والطرائق في سياق واحد، بحجة أن العقل - مثلاً - هو وحده الذي يمكن أن يكون مصدراً للمعرفة ووسيلة لها بآن واحد لأنه مصدر للمعارف الغريزية أو الفطرية المركوزة في الإنسان، ووسيلة نحصل بواسطتها على المعلومة من المصدر، بالفكر والنظر الطويل.

ويخالفه باحث آخر يعتبر أن معارف الوحي والكون لا يتوصل إليهما إلا بفعل التفكر والتدبر والتعقل والعقل ليس عضواً في الإنسان بل هو طاقة وقدرة وعمل. ولم يرد في القرآن إلا بصيغة الفعل لا بصيغة الاسم. ويتساءل مستنكراً: فلو كان العقل مصدراً للعلم ومطلعاً فما الوسيلة التي يستخرج أو يتوصل بها إلى العلم من هذا المصدر.

وخروجاً من الإلباس الذي قد يؤدي إلى عدم التحديد الواضح للمراد بمصطلحي المصادر والطرائق في هذه الدراسة، فإن الضرورة الموضوعية تقتضي المسارعة هنا إلى الإفصاح عن أنه أياً ما كان المقصود لدى الباحثين الآخرين من إطلاقهم مصطلحي المصادر والطرائق فإن المراد هنا من إطلاقهما: الأصول التي يستقي منها الباحث المسلم معرفته، والأدوات التي يتعامل بها مع الظواهر المختلفة فيما يخص الوحي والحس والعقل فحسب. أما الحدس والإلهام والرؤيا الصادقة فمصادر ثانوية لا تتجاوز ذلك إلى الطرائق لأنها ذاتية داخلية ليست في متناول إرادة الإنسان، كما هو الحال مع المصادر والطرائق الرئيسة. و مستند هذا الاصطلاح بعض الإشارات القرآنية التي جمعت بين الوحي بوصفه مصدراً وأداة كإطار موجه يوصل الملتزم بمرجعيته إلى تلك المعارف، هذه الآيات: ﴿قُلْ إِنما أُنْذِرُكُمْ بِالْوَخي... [الأنبياء: ٤٥/٢١]، ﴿وَأَنذِرْ بِهِ الَّذِينَ يَخافُونَ أَن يُحْشَرُوا إِلَى رَبِّهِمْ... [الأنعام: ٥١/٦، ﴿وَذَكِّرْ بِهِ أَنْ تُبْسَلَ نَفْسٌ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ... [الأنعام: ٧٠/٦]، وما كُنْتَ تَدْرِي مَا الْكِتَابُ وَلَا الإيمان وَلَكِنْ جَعَلْنَاهُ نُوراً نَهْدِي بِهِ مَنْ نَشَاءُ مِنْ عِبادِنا [الشورى:٥٢/٤٢]. ومـن جانب آخر فإن العقل يحمل في ثناياه قوانين فطرية أصيلة ممـا تعـده مصدراً بـلا تجاوز، وإن كان أداة في غالب وظائفه. وحاصل القول: إن ألفاظا من مثل الوحي والعقل هي من قبيل المصطلحات التي بينها اجتماع وافتراق فتجتمع إذا هي افترقت وتفترق إذا هي اجتمعت على النحو الذي سبق بيانه عند الحديث عن العلم والمعرفة.

ثانيًا: خلط بين المصادر والطرائق وبعض عناصر النظرية

نظراً لعدم تحرير بعض الباحثين لمصطلحات دراساتهم فقد وقع بعضهم فيما يعدّ خلطاً أدّى إلى إلباس بين المصادر والميادين مثلاً. كالقول بأن الأنفس والآفاق هي مصادر المعرفة. ومنهم من يعزو إلى طوائف من المتكلمين (المعتزلة والأشاعرة) اعتبارهم العقل ميدانا للمعرفة.
Chapter Three - Introduction

Taymiyya in the cognitive system, and he described it as integration between the sources of knowledge: revelation, intellect, and senses. And in a later place, he expressed with sources and means together as two names for a single referent, saying:

'Indeed, dropping any source or means of knowledge (revelation, intellect, or senses) or making it an alternative or counterpart to another will cause much intellectual harm to knowledge. But there is one who sees a difference between the two terms, so the sources and means cannot be summarized in one context, arguing that the intellect - for example - is alone that which can be a source of knowledge and a means for it at the same time, because it is a source for instinctive or innate knowledge embedded in man, and a means by which we obtain information from the source, through thought and prolonged reflection.

And another researcher disagrees with him, considering that the knowledge of revelation and the universe cannot be reached except through the act of thinking, pondering, and reasoning, and that the intellect is not an organ in man but rather an energy, capacity, and action. And it has not been mentioned in the Quran except in the form of a verb, not in the form of a noun. And he asks disapprovingly: If the intellect were a source of knowledge and an origin, then what is the means by which knowledge is extracted or reached from this source?

And to escape the confusion that may lead to a lack of clear determination of what is intended by the terms sources and means in this study, objective necessity requires hastening here to declare that whatever is intended by other researchers in their usage of the two terms, what is intended here by their usage is: the foundations from which the Muslim researcher derives his knowledge, and the tools with which he deals with various phenomena concerning revelation, senses, and intellect only. As for intuition, inspiration, and true dreams, they are secondary sources that do not extend to being means, because they are subjective and internal, not within the reach of human will, as is the case with the main sources and means. And the basis of this terminology is some Quranic indications that combine revelation as a source and an instrument as a guiding framework that delivers the one committed to its authority to those types of knowledge. These verses: 'Say, I only warn you by revelation...' [Al-Anbiya 21:45], 'And warn thereby those who fear that they will be gathered to their Lord...' [Al-An'am 6:51], 'And remind thereby lest a soul be delivered to destruction by what it has earned...' [Al-An'am 6:70], 'And you did not know what the Book is nor faith, but We made it a light by which We guide whom We will of Our servants' [Ash-Shura 42:52]. And on the other hand, the intellect carries within it genuine innate laws, which makes it a source without doubt, even though it is a tool in most of its functions. And the conclusion: words like revelation and intellect are types of terms that have both commonality and difference, so they come together when they separate and separate when they come together in the manner previously explained when discussing knowledge and cognition.

Second: Confusion between sources and means and some elements of theory

Due to some researchers not refining the terms of their studies, some of them have fallen into what is considered a confusion that led to ambiguity between sources and fields, for example. Such as saying that the souls and horizons are sources of knowledge. And some attribute to groups of theologians (the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya) considering the intellect as a field of knowledge.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological clarification and critique. The author begins by referencing Ibn Taymiyya's description of the cognitive system as integrating revelation, intellect, and senses. He then notes that some scholars use 'sources' and 'means' interchangeably, but others see a distinction. The author presents two opposing views: one holds that the intellect alone can be both source and means, while another argues that intellectual knowledge of revelation and the universe requires active thinking and that the intellect is not a passive source but an active capacity. The author then resolves the terminological confusion by defining 'sources' (masadir) as the foundations from which knowledge is derived—specifically revelation, senses, and intellect—and 'means' (tara'iq) as the tools for dealing with phenomena. He distinguishes secondary sources (intuition, inspiration, true dreams) as subjective and not under human control, thus not serving as means. He supports this with Quranic verses showing revelation as both source and guiding tool. He also acknowledges that the intellect contains innate laws, making it a source in some respects, though primarily a tool. Finally, he criticizes researchers who confuse sources with fields (mayadin), such as mistakenly calling the souls and horizons (anfus wa afaq) sources, or attributing to the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya the view that reason is a field of knowledge. The passage underscores the need for precise terminology in Islamic epistemology.

الطرائق
الوحي
العقل
الحس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is classified as a secondary source that does not serve as a means of knowledge?
A) Revelation (al-wahy)
B) The senses (al-hiss)
C) True dreams (al-ru'ya al-sadiqa)
D) The intellect (al-'aql)

Key Takeaway
Sources are the foundations of knowledge; means are the tools—only primary sources (revelation, senses, intellect) serve as both, while secondary sources like intuition remain mere sources.

?

Critique of Conflation Between Sources and Methods
Author critiques researchers who confuse sources with fields or reduce all sources to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical errors of conflating sources with methods and of oversimplifying the source-revelation relationship in Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Confusing the ultimate source (God) with the medium (revelation) undermines knowledge theory.

In this lesson, we examine a critical passage from a work on Quranic epistemology. The author points out several common mistakes made by researchers: confusing sources of knowledge with fields or methods, and reducing all sources to God while treating revelation as merely a tool. This conflation and oversimplification have serious consequences for how we understand the structure of knowledge in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflation types
Read the passage and list the two main errors the author identifies: confusing sources with fields (e.g., calling 'the selves and horizons' sources) and reducing all sources to God alone.
The author gives examples like 'مصادر المعرفة' (sources of knowledge) vs. 'الميادين' (fields).
2
Analyze the reduction to God
The author criticizes those who make God the sole source of knowledge, then treat revelation as a 'tool'. Formulate the logical question the author raises: if God is the source, how can revelation be a tool? Do we receive knowledge from God except through revelation?
3
Trace the author's alternative
The author mentions a researcher who explains that all knowledge originates from God, and sense and intellect are part of His creation. This resolves the conflation by affirming God as ultimate source while recognizing mediums.
This alternative avoids both conflation and oversimplification.
4
Assess internal consistency
Check whether the author's critique itself uses valid reasoning. Does the author commit any fallacy? Note that the critique is based on the logical principle that means and end cannot be conflated.
The critique is coherent and raises a genuine epistemological distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيًا: خلط بين المصادر والطرائق وبعض عناصر النظرية. نظراً لعدم تحرير بعض الباحثين لمصطلحات دراساتهم فقد وقع بعضهم فيما يعدّ خلطاً أدّى إلى إلباس بين المصادر والميادين مثلاً. كالقول بأن الأنفس والآفاق هي مصادر المعرفة. ومنهم من يعزو إلى طوائف من المتكلمين (المعتزلة والأشاعرة) اعتبارهم العقل ميدانا للمعرفة. ومن جهة أخرى فإن بعضهم يميل إلى الإجمال في ذكر مصدر المعرفة، فيجعله واحداً ممثلاً في (الله) سبحانه وتعالى. وإنما تم وصف ذلك بأنه ميل إلى الإجمال لأنهم لا يعنون بذلك الحصر، بدليل أن بعضهم عاد في موضع آخر فذكر الوحي، تحت عنوان (أدوات المعرفة). والسؤال المنطقي: كيف يكون (الله) مصدراً للمعرفة، والوحي أداة لها؟ وهل نستقي من الله - جل جلاله - معرفتنا إلا عن طريق وحيه لنبيه ممثلاً في القرآن الكريم قبل أي مصدر آخر؟ وهذا السؤال عينه يطرح على من ميّز بين (الله) - جل جلاله - وبين الوحي القرآن الكريم وعده مصدرًا ثانيًا لها. ترى هل هناك ثمة فرق أيضاً؟ أو ليس الله تعالى هو مصدر المعرفة وقد أوحى بالقرآن إلى نبيه محمد - - بواحدة من صور الوحي الثلاث: وما كانَ لِبَشَرِ أَنْ يُكَلِّمَهُ اللهُ إِلَّا وَحْيا أَوْ مِنْ وَرَاءِ حِجَابٍ أَوْ يُرْسِلَ رَسُولاً فَيُوحِيَ بِإِذْنِهِ مَا يَشَاءُ [الشورى: ٥١/٤٢]. وإلى هذا المعنى الذي تمت الإشارة إليه من كون هناك إجمال في ذكر مصدر كلي هو الله تعالى ذهب أحد الباحثين في تفسيره لهذا المعنى إلى أن أصل المعارف كلها يعود إلى الله - سبحانه - فهو الذي خلق وعلّم. وما الحس والعقل إلا جزءا من مخلوقات الله - تعالى - ولذلك فالإنسان مدين لله - تعالى - في أصل خلقه، وعارف مدين لله - تعالى - في أصل معرفته.
Second: Conflation between sources and methods and some elements of the theory. Due to some researchers not refining the terminology of their studies, some fell into what is considered a conflation that led to confusion between sources and fields, for example, such as saying that the selves and the horizons are sources of knowledge. And among them are those who attribute to sects of theologians (Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya) considering the intellect a field of knowledge. On the other hand, some tend to be general in mentioning the source of knowledge, making it one represented in Allah, glorified and exalted. This is described as a tendency to generality because they do not mean by it exclusivity, as evidenced by the fact that some of them returned elsewhere and mentioned revelation under the title (tools of knowledge). The logical question: How can Allah be a source of knowledge, and revelation a tool for it? Do we derive our knowledge from Allah - exalted be He - except through His revelation to His prophet, represented in the Noble Quran, before any other source? And this same question is posed to those who distinguished between Allah - exalted be He - and the noble Quran and considered it a second source. Do you see any difference also? Or is not Allah the source of knowledge, and He revealed the Quran to His prophet Muhammad - one of the three forms of revelation: 'And it is not for any human that Allah should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a veil or by sending a messenger to reveal by His permission what He wills' [Ash-Shura: 51]. And to this meaning that was referred to of there being a generality in mentioning a universal source that is Allah, one researcher went in his interpretation of this meaning to the effect that the origin of all knowledge returns to Allah - glorified - for He created and taught. And sense and intellect are only part of Allah's creations - exalted - therefore man is indebted to Allah - exalted - in the origin of his creation, and knowledgeable is indebted to Allah - exalted - in the origin of his knowledge.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage critiques various errors made by researchers regarding the classification of sources and methods. First, it points out a conflation between sources and fields, exemplified by calling 'the selves and horizons' sources of knowledge. Second, it notes that some attribute to Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya the view that intellect is a field of knowledge. Third, it criticizes those who reduce all sources to God alone, arguing that this is an oversimplification because they later refer to revelation as a tool. The author raises a logical question: if God is the source, how can revelation be merely a tool, since we receive knowledge from God only through revelation? The same objection applies to those who distinguish God from the Quran as a second source. Finally, it mentions a researcher who explains that all knowledge originates from God, and sense and intellect are part of His creation, so man is indebted to God in both creation and knowledge.

D) They contradict the Quran by denying revelation

Key Takeaway
Precise terminology distinguishing ultimate source (God) from means (revelation, sense, intellect) is essential to avoid logical contradictions in Islamic epistemology.

Avoiding Anachronism in Quranic Interpretation: The Case of 'Sam''
Author critiques the interpretation of 'sam'' as revelation and argues it means physical hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify anachronistic readings of Quranic terms and apply the principle of original linguistic usage (al-wad' al-lughawi) to determine a term's intended meaning.

Core Idea
Quranic terms must be read in their original sense, not later technical jargon.

This lesson examines a critique of interpreting 'sam'' (hearing) in Quranic verses as revelation. The author, drawing on Zamakhshari, warns against anachronism—reading later theological concepts back into the Quran. You will learn to distinguish literal from technical usage and apply this principle in tafsir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the researcher's claim
The passage begins by noting that a certain researcher interprets 'sam'' in three Quranic verses as meaning 'transmission' (naql) or 'revelation' (wahy). This is the target claim.
The researcher is not named, but the approach is common among those who see 'sam'' as a source of religious knowledge.
2
Examine the Quranic verses cited
The verses from Surah Qaf, Hajj, and Mulk pair 'sam'' with 'heart' (qalb) or 'intellect' ('aql) in contexts of admonition. Read them closely to see if the context demands a literal or metaphorical sense.
For example, in Hajj 46: 'ears with which they hear' explicitly mentions the physical organ.
3
Analyze Zamakhshari's critique
Zamakhshari tentatively mentions the interpretation that 'sam'' and 'aql' refer to evidence from revelation and reason, but he immediately dismisses it as an innovation. His argument: it would absurdly suggest the verses were revealed after the emergence of later schools (ahl al-hadith and ahl al-ra'y).
This shows that reading later technical terms into the Quran is logically problematic.
4
Assess the author's argument about terminological evolution
The author adds that 'sam'' as a term for revelation is a later development from the second century sectarian conflicts. Therefore, it cannot be the intended meaning in the Quran, which uses the word in its original sense.
This is a key epistemological principle: technical terms evolve, and one must avoid retrojection.
5
Conclude the original meaning
Based on the linguistic evidence and the explicit phrase 'ears with which they hear', the author concludes that 'sam'' in these verses means physical hearing (one of the five senses), not revelation.
This conclusion is reinforced by the pairing with 'heart' and 'intellect', which are also literal faculties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصنيفه المصادر المعرفة هذا المذهب، ولكنه يلجأ إلى بعض الآيات محتجا بها لدعم وجهة نظره، ومن تلك الآيات:
وَإِنَّ فِي ذَلِكَ لَذِكْرَى لِمَنْ كَانَ لَهُ قَلْبٌ أَوْ أَلْقَى السَّمْعَ وَهُوَ شَهِيدٌ [ق: 37/50].
أَفَلَمْ يَسِيرُوا فِي الْأَرْضِ فَتَكُونَ لَهُمْ قُلُوبٌ يَعْقِلُونَ بِها أَوْ آذَانٌ يَسْمَعُونَ بها [الحج: 46/22].
وَقالُوا لَوْ كُنَّا نَسْمَعُ أَوْ نَعْقِلُ ما كُنا فِي أَصْحابِ السَّعِيرِ [الملك: 10/67].
ونجد الباحث يحمل السمع في تلك الآيات على معنى النقل أو الوحي. وعلى الرغم من أنّ بعض المفسرين قد أشار إلى شيء من ذلك بصيغة التمريض كقول الزمخشري في تفسير آية سورة الملك المشار إليها: وقيل إنما جمع بين السمع والعقل لأن مدار التكليف على أدلة السمع والعقل. لكنه أعقب ذلك بقوله: ومن بدع التفاسير أنّ المراد لو كنا على مذهب أصحاب الحديث أو على مذهب أصحاب الرأي، كأن هذه الآية نزلت بعد ظهور هذين المذهبين وكأن سائر أصحاب المذاهب والمجتهدين قد أنزل الله وعيدهم، وكأن من كان من هؤلاء فهو من الناجين لا محالة. وعدة المبشرين من الصحابة عشرة لم يضم إليهم حادي عشر، كأن من يجوز على الصراط أكثرهم لم يسمعوا باسم هذين الفريقين.
إن تعقيب الزمخشري حق، ومثله أن يقال: إن مصطلح السمع تعبيراً عن (النقل) أو (الوحي) مصطلح حادث لم يعرف في عهد النبوة، وإنما ظهر إبان حقبة النزاع الذي برزت جذوره بين الفرق الإسلامية ابتداءً من القرن الثاني. وعلى ذلك فإن السمع في تلك الآيات لا يحمل سوى معنى العضو الحسي المعروف، وهو واحد من خمس حواس معلومة خص بالذكر لما يمتاز به من بين سائر الحواس وخاصة البصر، كما سنبين ذلك عند الحديث عن الحس بإذن الله - تعالى -. ولعله مما يؤكد هذا المعنى قوله تعالى في آية سورة الحج: أو آذانٌ يَسْمَعُونَ بِها [الحج: 46/22].
His classification of the sources of knowledge of this school, but he resorts to some Quranic verses, using them as evidence to support his point of view. Among those verses: 'Indeed in that is a reminder for whoever has a heart or who listens while present' [Qaf: 37/50]. 'Have they not traveled through the land so that they have hearts by which to reason and ears by which to hear?' [Hajj: 46/22]. 'And they say, 'If only we had been listening or reasoning, we would not be among the companions of the Blaze'' [Mulk: 10/67]. And we find the researcher carrying the meaning of 'sam'' in those verses as transmission or revelation. Although some commentators have indicated something of that in a tentative manner, like Zamakhshari's statement in interpreting the verse of Surat al-Mulk mentioned: 'And it was said that hearing and intellect were combined because the basis of obligation is evidence of hearing and intellect.' But he followed that with his statement: 'It is among the innovations of interpretation that the intended meaning is that we were upon the madhhab of the people of hadith or upon the madhhab of the people of opinion, as if this verse was revealed after the emergence of these two madhhabs, and as if all other holders of madhhabs and mujtahids have been sent down Allah's threat, and as if whoever is among these [two groups] is among the saved without doubt. The number of those given glad tidings among the Companions is ten, with no eleventh added to them, as if most of those who cross the Sirat have not heard of these two groups.' Indeed, Zamakhshari's comment is correct. And similarly it can be said that the term 'sam'' as an expression for 'transmission' or 'revelation' is a later term that was not known in the era of prophethood; rather, it emerged during the era of conflict whose roots appeared among the Islamic sects starting from the second century. Accordingly, 'sam'' in those verses carries only the meaning of the well-known sensory organ, which is one of the five known senses, specifically mentioned because of its distinction among the other senses, especially sight, as we will explain when discussing the senses, God willing. And perhaps confirming this meaning is His statement in the verse of Surat al-Hajj: 'or ears with which they hear' [Hajj: 46/22].
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage critiques the interpretation of the Quranic term 'sam'' (hearing) as referring to revelation or transmitted knowledge. The author begins by noting that a certain researcher uses Quranic verses that mention 'sam'' to support his view that 'sam'' means transmission or revelation. The verses cited are from Surat Qaf, Hajj, and Mulk, each pairing 'sam'' with 'qalb' (heart) or 'aql' (intellect) in contexts of reflection and admonition. The author then invokes the commentary of Zamakhshari, who cautiously mentions an interpretation that 'sam'' and 'aql' refer to evidence from revelation and reason, but Zamakhshari immediately criticizes such an interpretation as an innovation, arguing that it absurdly suggests the verses were revealed after the emergence of the traditionalist and rationalist schools. The author endorses Zamakhshari's refutation and adds that the term 'sam'' as a technical term for revelation is a later development from the second century Islamic sectarian conflicts. Consequently, the original meaning of 'sam'' in these verses is the physical sense of hearing, one of the five senses. This is reinforced by the end of the Hajj verse which specifies 'ears with which they hear'. The passage is an epistemological and terminological clarification, warning against reading later technical meanings into Quranic usage.

Key Terms
السمع
النقل
الوحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Intellect ('aql)

Ibn Taymiyya's Five Sources of Knowledge
Author presents Ibn Taymiyya's five sources of knowledge and their application in Islamic education.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's five sources of knowledge and explain which types of knowledge each source is best suited for.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya expands knowledge sources to sensation, reason, revelation, intuition, and inspiration, with revelation supreme.

Ibn Taymiyya is known for his trichotomy of knowledge sources (reason, revelation, sense), but here he elaborates a fivefold classification that includes intuitive and inspired knowledge. This passage defines each source and its domain, emphasizing their cooperative integration under revelation's authority. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for grasping Islamic epistemology's breadth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five sources
List the five sources mentioned: al-hiss (sensation), al-'aql (reason), al-wahy (revelation), and al-ilham (inspiration). Note that 'hiss' here is an inner sense, not the five external senses.
The passage also implies a fifth source from the context: 'ma yaqdhifuhu Allah fi al-qalb' which is sensation as inner perception.
2
Understand the definition of each source
For each source, note its description: sensation is what God casts into the heart; reason is rational reflection; revelation is truthful divine report; inspiration is knowledge compelled without acquisition.
Compare the expanded definition of 'hiss' with classical Peripatetic usage.
3
Match sources to knowledge domains
The passage assigns each source to a type of knowledge: sensation for sensory knowledge, reason for rational knowledge, intuition/inspiration for taste/emotional knowledge, revelation for religious, moral, and unseen knowledge.
Revelation is given absolute authority in the unseen world.
4
Explore the integrative role of revelation
Although all sources cooperate, revelation is the ultimate authority, especially concerning the unseen. This hierarchy ensures coherence among the sources.
5
Reflect on the educational implication
Each source functions educationally in its sphere, suggesting a holistic curriculum that addresses sensory, rational, emotional, and spiritual dimensions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصنيف الخماسي
ونجد هذا التصنيف مفصلاً عند ابن تيمية. فعلى حين سبق أن قرر ثلاثية المصادر والطرائق لكنه عنى بذلك المصادر والطرائق الأساسية للمعرفة فحسب. ويتضح هذا من خلال إيراده المصادر وطرائق أخرى. ففي معرض حديثه عن كون بعض أهل السنة قد احتجوا على أن المعرفة والإيمان يحصلان للعبد بفضل ورحمته وهدايته وتعريفه يؤكد ابن تيمية على وحدة تلك المصادر والطرائق جنباً إلى جنب من غير استحالة حصول المعرفة بالنظر العقلي (العقل) أو عـدم حصولها بتعليم الرسول والعلماء والمؤمنين ودعائهم وبيانهم واستدلالهم، بل يحصل ذلك تارة بالإشارة إلى الدليل العقلي (العقل)، وتارة بالإخبار بالحق (الوحي)، وتارة بما يقذفه الله في قلب المرء من النظر والاعتبار والاستدلال معنا (الحس)، وتارة باضطراره إلى العلم من غير اكتساب منه (الإلهام). وقد تابع هذا التصنيف أحد الباحثين في ميدان التربية الإسلامية، كمـا مـال إلى الفصل بين المصادر والطرائق الأساسية وتلك المساعدة، كما مر آنفاً عند الحديث عن التصنيف الثنائي. ويبقى التأكيد على أن كل واحد من هذه الخمسة يعمل تربوياً في مجال معرفته. فالحس يتناسب والمعارف الحسية، والعقل يتناسب والمعارف العقلية والحدس والإلهام يتناسبان والمعارف الذوقية والعاطفية، والوحي يتناسب والمعارف الدينية والخلقية والغيبية بصورة عامة. وبصورة جامعة فإن جميع تلك المصادر والطرائق تؤدي وظائفها في ميدان المعرفة في عالم الشهادة، مثلما تؤديها في ميدان عالم الغيب على نحو تكاملي، وإن كانت المرجعية المطلقة فيه للوحي. ومما هو حري بالتنويه هنا أنّ من التعسف الجزم بصحة واحد من هذه التصنيفات
The Fivefold Classification. And we find this classification detailed with Ibn Taymiyya. For while he had previously established a trichotomy of sources and methods, he meant by that only the basic sources and methods of knowledge. And this becomes clear through his mention of other sources and methods. For in the context of his discussion that some of the Ahl al-Sunna argued that knowledge and faith come to the servant by virtue of God's favor, mercy, guidance, and definition, Ibn Taymiyya affirms the unity of those sources and methods side by side without knowledge being impossible to obtain by rational reflection (reason) or not being obtained by the teaching of the Messenger, the scholars, and the believers, and their supplication, explanation, and argumentation. Rather, it is obtained sometimes by reference to rational evidence (reason), sometimes by informing of the truth (revelation), sometimes by what God casts into the heart of the person of reflection, consideration, and reasoning with us (sensation), and sometimes by his being compelled to knowledge without acquisition (inspiration). And one researcher in the field of Islamic education followed this classification, as he tended to separate between basic sources and methods and auxiliary ones, as mentioned earlier in the discussion of the twofold classification. And the emphasis remains that each of these five works educationally in its field of knowledge. So sensation is appropriate for sensory knowledges, and reason is appropriate for rational knowledges, and intuition and inspiration are appropriate for taste-based and emotional knowledges, and revelation is appropriate for religious, moral, and unseen knowledges in general. And in a comprehensive manner, all those sources and methods perform their functions in the field of knowledge in the world of witness, just as they perform them in the field of the unseen world in an integrative manner, even though absolute authority in it belongs to revelation. And it is worth noting here that it is arbitrary to assert the correctness of one of these classifications.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage details Ibn Taymiyya's fivefold classification of sources and methods of knowledge. It begins by noting that Ibn Taymiyya previously affirmed a trichotomy (triple division) of basic sources, but here expands to five. The core text from Ibn Taymiyya is presented: he affirms the unity of multiple sources—rational evidence (reason), truthful report (revelation), divinely inspired reflection (sensation), and compelled knowledge (inspiration). Sensation here is described as what God casts into the heart (an inner sensing or intuition). The author then notes that these five sources (sensation, reason, revelation, intuition/inspiration) are each suited to different types of knowledge: sensory knowledge for sensation, rational for reason, taste/emotional for intuition/inspiration, and religious/moral/unseen for revelation. The passage concludes by emphasizing the integrative cooperation of these sources, with revelation holding ultimate authority, and warns against asserting any one classification as absolute. This is definitional, as it defines each source and its domain. In the Islamic tradition, Ibn Taymiyya's classification is notable for including inspiration as a distinct source alongside reason and revelation.

Key Terms
الحس
العقل
الوحي
الإلهام

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's fivefold classification integrates sensation, reason, revelation, intuition, and inspiration, with revelation as the ultimate authority, each source serving a distinct cognitive domain.

Defining Sensationalism and Its Proponents
Author defines sensationalism and lists its proponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the core claim of sensationalism and list three of its historical proponents as presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Sensationalism claims all knowledge comes only through the senses.

This passage defines sensationalism, an epistemological school that argues the senses are the sole source of all knowledge and the final judges of its value. It contrasts this view with rationalism and identifies Hobbes, Condillac, and Hume as key proponents. Understanding this modern school is important for comparing it with the Islamic view of knowledge, which recognizes both sensory and rational sources.

Guided Path
1
Locate the core claim
Find the sentence that states sensationalism's position on the source of knowledge. Note that it says 'الحواس هي المصدر الوحيد لجميع معارفنا' (the senses are the sole source of all our knowledge).
2
Identify the proponents
List the names mentioned: Hobbes, Condillac, Hume. Also note de Gérando as the possible coiner of the term.
3
Understand the reduction of the rational to the sensible
What does 'يرد المعقول إلى المحسوس' mean? It means rational knowledge is either sensations or reducible to them.
4
Contrast with rationalism
The passage mentions that sensationalism is opposed to rationalism. Consider what rationalism claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نشأ عن الاعتماد على وسيلة الحس ماعرف بالمذهب الحسي (Sensationalism) وهو مذهب يرى أن الحواس هي المصدر الوحيد لجميع معارفنا، وهي وحدها التي تفصل في قيمة هذه المعارف. وهذا المذهب يرد المعقول إلى المحسوس، ومن أظهر القائلين به هوبز وكوندياك وهيوم... ويقابل العقلانية (Rationalism). و معنى رده المعقول إلى المحسوس رؤيته إلى المعرفة على أنها إما إحساسات أو راجعة إلى إحساسات. ويُرجح أن دي جيراندو هو الذي ابتدع اصطلاح المذهب الحسي.
And from reliance on the means of sensation arose what is known as sensationalism, a doctrine that holds that the senses are the sole source of all our knowledge, and they alone decide the value of this knowledge. This doctrine reduces the rational to the sensible, and among its most prominent proponents are Hobbes, Condillac, and Hume... And it is contrasted with rationalism. And the meaning of its reduction of the rational to the sensible is its view of knowledge as either sensations or reducible to sensations. And it is likely that de Gérando was the one who originated the term sensationalism.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines sensationalism as an epistemological school that makes the senses the exclusive source and arbiter of all knowledge. The author explains that sensationalism reduces rational concepts to sensory experiences, listing Hobbes, Condillac, and Hume as major advocates. The passage also notes that sensationalism is opposed to rationalism and that de Gérando likely coined the term. The definition is presented in an expository style, characteristic of modern philosophical analysis, rather than the classical logic tradition.

Key Terms
المذهب الحسي (al-madhhab al-hissi): The doctrine that all knowledge originates from the senses and that the senses are the sole judge of knowledge. [Note: This is a modern philosophical term; in classical Islamic logic, al-hiss refers to external senses as one source of knowledge, not an exclusive one.]
الحس (al-hiss): The faculty of sensation, the means by which external objects are perceived. [Note: In classical logic, al-hiss includes both external and internal senses, but here it is used narrowly for external perception.]
المعقول (al-ma'qul): What is grasped by the intellect, as opposed to what is sensed. [Note: In classical logic, al-ma'qul often refers to universals and intelligibles; here it means rational knowledge.]

Distinguishing Sensation from Empiricism: The Domain of Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author distinguishes between sensationalism and empiricism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given epistemological claim belongs to the domain of sensation (tasawwur) or empiricism (tasdiq) and explain the distinction using the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
Sensation provides concepts; empiricism provides judgments.

This passage from a modern Islamic epistemology text clarifies a common conflation: sensationalism and empiricism are not identical. The author uses classical logical terms—tasawwur and tasdiq—to draw a precise boundary. You will learn to apply this distinction to evaluate epistemological claims in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions being compared
Locate the terms 'الحس' (sensation) and 'التجريب' (empiricism) in the Arabic. Note that the author initially presents sensationalism as a form of empiricism but then highlights differences, especially regarding the active role of the mind.
Both deny innate ideas but differ in the mind's function.
2
Extract the criterion for distinction
The author places sensation in the domain of 'التصور' (tasawwur, conceptualization) and empiricism in 'التصديق' (tasdiq, assent). Sensation involves simple perception; empiricism involves judgment, composition, and inference from experience.
Tasawwur is grasping the form without judgment; tasdiq is affirming or denying a proposition.
3
Apply the distinction to an example
Take a statement like 'The world is created.' The concept 'world' is a tasawwur; the judgment 'is created' is a tasdiq. Sensation provides the concept, while empiricism (or other sources) provides the judgment.
Practice with examples from kalam: 'existence' as tasawwur, 'Allah exists' as tasdiq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين الحس والتجريب. يرى بعض الباحثين أن المذهب الحسي ما هو إلا إحدى صور التجريبية (Empiricism). إذ هو يقوم على أن الحس باب المعرفة الوحيد، وليس في العقل شيء لم يمر بالحس ابتداء. ولهذا فإنهم ينكرون أن يولد العقل مزوداً بأفكار فطرية موروثة كما يرى العقليون. ووفق هذا المفهوم فإن التجريبيين يلتقون مع الحسيين، ولكنهم يختلفون عنهم في كون التجريبيين يرون أن فاعلية العقل تبدو في عملياته العقلية العليا كالتفكير فيما تنقله إليه الحواس من صور ذهنية، وتأليف أفكار من العناصر التي استمدها من التجربة، وهي أفكار لا وجود لها في العالم الخارجي. ولكن لئن أرجع التجريبيون المعرفة إلى التجربة، وسواء كان ذلك مصدراً وحيداً للمعرفة، أم أساساً لها على الأقل، فذلك أيضاً هو مذهب الحسيين بجامع التقاء الطرفين في اعتبار الإدراك الخارجي، أي عن طريق الحواس أصل كل علم، ولكن لكون الحس أمراً يشمل الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة، فإن إدراك الأشياء الخارجية يسمى إحساساً، في حين يسمى إدراك الأشياء الباطنة تأملاً. والتعبير الأدق أن المذهب الحسي يعدّ جميع المعارف قائمة على إدراك الحواس، ومن ثم ينقلها إلى العقل، أما الاتجاه التجريبي فيعد التجربة مصدراً وحيداً للمعرفة، وذلك من خلال المدركات الحسية. ومن هنا ندرك الصلة بين الحسية والتجريبية من زاوية كون الأولى تدخل في محال التصور، في حين تدخل الأخرى في محال التصديق، مع الأخذ في الاعتبار أن الحس يقوم على عنصري الملاحظة والتجربة، والفرق بينهما أن الملاحظة تقتصر على مشاهدة الأشياء على ما هي عليه في الطبيعة، بينما لابد أن يتدخل الباحث في مجال بحث التجربة.
The difference between sensation and empiricism. Some researchers see that sensationalism is but one form of empiricism. For it is based on the idea that sensation is the sole door of knowledge, and there is nothing in the mind that did not first pass through sensation. Hence they deny that the mind is born equipped with innate inherited ideas as rationalists hold. According to this concept, empiricists agree with sensationalists, but differ in that empiricists see that the activity of the mind appears in its higher mental operations, like thinking about the mental images conveyed to it by the senses, and composing ideas from the elements derived from experience, ideas that have no existence in the external world. But if empiricists trace knowledge back to experience, whether as the sole source of knowledge or at least as its foundation, that is also the doctrine of the sensationalists, in that both parties agree in considering external perception, i.e. via the senses, as the origin of all knowledge. However, because sensation includes both external and internal senses, the perception of external things is called sensation, while the perception of internal things is called reflection. The more precise expression is that sensationalism considers all knowledge to be based on sense perception, then transfers it to the mind, whereas the empirical approach considers experience as the sole source of knowledge, through sense perceptions. From here we grasp the connection between sensation and empiricism from the angle that the former falls into the domain of concept (tasawwur), while the latter falls into the domain of assent (tasdiq), taking into account that sensation is based on the two elements of observation and experiment, and the difference between them is that observation is limited to watching things as they are in nature, while the researcher must intervene in the field of experiment.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage clarifies the relationship and differences between sensationalism and empiricism. It begins by noting that some consider sensationalism a form of empiricism, as both deny innate ideas and base knowledge on sense experience. However, the author explains that empiricists emphasize the mind's active role in forming ideas from sensory data, which sensationalists do not stress. The key distinction is that sensationalism focuses on perception of external objects (sensation) and internal states (reflection), while empiricism emphasizes experience as the sole source of knowledge. The author then introduces a distinction from Islamic logic: sensationalism pertains to tasawwur (simple conceptualization), while empiricism pertains to tasdiq (judgment or assent). This is a crucial epistemological refinement. Finally, the author differentiates between observation (observing nature) and experiment (intervention), noting that sensation includes both.

Key Terms
التصور
التصديق
التجريبية
الحس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, sensationalism falls into the domain of:
A) Tasdiq
B) Tasawwur
C) Both tasawwur and tasdiq
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Sensationalism pertains to simple conceptualization (tasawwur), while empiricism pertains to judgment (tasdiq).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a concept (tasawwur) and a judgment (tasdiq) in the following statement: 'Riba is forbidden because it involves injustice.' Explain which part is tasawwur and which is tasdiq.
?
Take the notion of 'tawhid' as a concept, and formulate a judgment about it that would be a tasdiq. Then explain how sensation and empiricism might relate to each in the formation of that judgment.

The Two Types of Senses: External and Internal
Author classifies senses into two types: external and internal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish external senses from internal senses based on their roles in sensory knowledge acquisition.

Core Idea
Senses are divided into two types: external (perception) and internal (reflection).

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of human senses into two categories. While the text is brief, the distinction between external and internal senses is crucial in Islamic epistemology for understanding how knowledge is acquired. The author asserts a consensus among scholars that senses are of only two types, setting the stage for a deeper exploration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The passage states that scholars agree the senses are of no more than two types. What is the claim being made?
This is a classification claim, not an argument.
2
Recall the implied classification
From the lesson title and key terms, infer that the two types are external (al-hawass al-zāhira) and internal (al-hawass al-bātina). External senses are the five classical senses; internal senses include faculties like common sense and memory.
The full list of internal senses is not given here.
3
Distinguish external from internal
External senses perceive the outer world directly (e.g., sight, hearing). Internal senses process and reflect on these perceptions, enabling recollection and abstraction.
This distinction is foundational in Islamic epistemology.
4
Connect to knowledge production
External senses provide raw data; internal senses transform it into concepts and judgments, leading to knowledge.
Both are necessary for empirical knowledge.

Sensory Knowledge and Locke's Critique of Innate Ideas
Author discusses senses' importance and Locke's refutation of innate ideas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for sensory knowledge certainty and reconstruct Locke's first argument against innate ideas.

Core Idea
Sensory knowledge is certain under conditions; Locke undermines rationalist innate ideas with an empirical counterexample.

This passage underscores the foundational role of the senses in acquiring knowledge, a theme that resonates deeply in Islamic epistemology. The author first details how sensory perception yields certainty when it is repeated, coherent among senses, and consistent with intellectual principles. He then introduces John Locke's empiricist challenge to rationalism: innate ideas cannot be universal because some human groups lack them. Understanding this debate helps you evaluate the epistemological basis of knowledge claims in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the role of senses in knowledge acquisition
Locate where the author states that most people rely solely on senses for knowledge and that sensory perceptions form most of their truths about the world.
This establishes the empirical foundation of everyday knowledge.
2
List the conditions for certainty of sensory knowledge
The author specifies three conditions: repeated sensory processes, agreement among different senses, and no contradiction with intellectual principles. Find these in the text.
These conditions prevent mistaken sensory reports.
3
Identify the challenge to intellectual principles
Note that some researchers reject the law of causality as a priori, grounding it instead in experience. This sets up the debate between rationalism and empiricism.
This is a key move in Locke's empiricism.
4
Extract Locke's first argument against innate ideas
Locke argues that if innate ideas were truly universal, all humans would possess them. But savage tribes lack them, so the claim of universality fails.
This is an empirical refutation of a rationalist doctrine.

أهمية الحواس تحتل الحواس - بوصفها مصدراً للمعرفة وطريقة لها - أهمية لا يقلل من قدرها باحث منصف. فالحس أحد منافذ المعرفة إلى نفس الإنسان، ويكاد المرء العادي لا يعترف بسواها وسيلة لمعرفته إذ إن معارفه كلها مبنية على ما رآه وسمعه ولمسه بنفسه. ومعظم حقائقه عن الدنيا الموضوعية والدنيا الخارجية المحيطة به إنما هي مدركات حسية. ولا شك أن المعرفة الحسية تكون يقينية عندما يُقدم الحس شهادته مؤكدة قاطعة بعد عمليات الإدراك الحسي المتواترة، والمتفقة مع شهادات الحواس الأخرى، وشهادات الآخرين من غير أن تتعارض مع أصل من أصول العقل وقوانينه، مع أن بعض الباحثين لا يسلّم حتى بقانون العلية أو قوانين العقل الأولية تلك، إذ إن العلاقة بين العلة والمعلول لا تقوم -حسب هذه الوجهة- على مبدأ عدم التناقض الصوري، الذي يذهب إليه العقليون بوصفه قانوناً حتمياً من قوانين العقل الأولية، وإنما على أساس التجربة. ومن قبل تزعم (جون لوك) لواء هذه المقولة في كتابه (مقالة في العقل البشري) فذهب إلى إنكار وجود أفكار أولية موروثة، أو مايسمى ببدائه العقول، كما يقول أصحاب المذهب العقلي. ويرد (لوك) على ما يُقدّم من أدلة تؤكد صحة دعوتهم بما يلي: أولاً : إن القول بأن هذه الأفكار مسلم بها من جميع الناس بلا استثناء محجوج بوجود قبائل متوحشة لا تعرفها.
The importance of senses: The senses—as a source of knowledge and a method for it—occupy an importance that no fair researcher diminishes. For sense is one of the entrances of knowledge into the human soul, and the ordinary person almost acknowledges no other means for his knowledge, since all his knowledge is built on what he has seen, heard, and touched himself. And most of his truths about the objective world and the external world surrounding him are sensory perceptions. There is no doubt that sensory knowledge becomes certain when the sense presents its testimony emphatically and decisively after repeated sensory perception processes, consistent with the testimonies of other senses and the testimonies of others, without contradicting any principle of the intellect and its laws. Although some researchers do not even accept the law of causality or those primary laws of the intellect, for the relationship between cause and effect—according to this view—does not rest on the principle of non-contradiction, which the rationalists hold as an inevitable law of the primary laws of the intellect, but rather on experience. And previously, John Locke championed this statement in his book 'An Essay Concerning Human Understanding', and he went to deny the existence of inherited primary ideas, or what is called the beginnings of intellects, as the adherents of the rationalist school say. And Locke refutes the evidence presented to confirm the validity of their claim as follows: First: The assertion that these ideas are accepted by all people without exception is refuted by the existence of savage tribes who do not know them.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage discusses the epistemological status of the senses. The author first affirms the fundamental importance of sensory knowledge, noting that most people rely on it exclusively. He then states that sensory knowledge can be certain when it meets conditions: repeated perception, consistency among senses, and no conflict with intellectual principles. He notes that some researchers reject even the law of causality, grounding it in experience rather than a priori reason. The author then introduces John Locke's rejection of innate ideas from the rationalist tradition. Locke's first argument is that the claim that innate ideas are universally accepted is disproven by the existence of savage tribes that lack such ideas. The passage thus presents a key empiricist critique of rationalism, focusing on the universality condition.

المعرفة الحسية
الأفكار الأولية

Checkpoint
What is Locke's first argument against innate ideas, as presented in the passage?
A) They are not known by animals.
B) Savage tribes exist who do not know them.
C) They contradict sensory experience.
D) They are not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Sensory knowledge becomes certain when it is repeated, coherent across senses, and consistent with intellectual principles; Locke refutes innate ideas by pointing to their absence in some human groups.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify the three conditions the author gives for sensory knowledge to be certain. Then, using those same criteria, evaluate whether a single eyewitness testimony in Islamic legal procedure (shahāda) meets the condition for certainty. What additional criteria might Islamic law add?
?
2. Locke's first argument against innate ideas is that they are not universally known. Imagine you are an Islamic rationalist (e.g., a follower of al-Fārābī). Write a brief counter-argument that defends the existence of self-evident truths (badāhi) despite Locke's objection. Use concepts from this lesson.

Conditions for Certainty of Sensory Knowledge
Conditions for sensory certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions that must be met for sensory knowledge to become certain according to the passage.

Core Idea
Sensory knowledge is certain only when it is repeated, consistent across senses and others, and does not contradict reason.

The passage begins by affirming the importance of the senses as a source of knowledge, but it immediately qualifies this by stating that sensory knowledge becomes certain only under specific conditions. Understanding these conditions is crucial for evaluating the reliability of empirical claims in Islamic epistemology, especially when assessing reports (khabar) or sensory evidence in theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The author states that the senses are a primary entrance of knowledge and that ordinary people rely almost exclusively on sensory experiences for their truths about the world.
2
Find the conditions for certainty
Look for the phrase 'when the sense presents its testimony in a decisive and conclusive manner after repeated sensory perception processes, in agreement with the testimonies of other senses and the testimonies of others, without conflicting with any principle of the principles of reason and its laws'.
This is a long sentence that contains three distinct conditions.
3
List the three conditions
Condition 1: Repeated sensory perceptions (al-Idrak al-hissi al-mutawatir). Condition 2: Agreement with other senses and testimonies of others. Condition 3: No conflict with rational principles (like the law of non-contradiction).
Each condition adds a layer of verification.
4
Compare with hadith criteria
Notice the similarity to the concept of tawatur in hadith: a report is certain if transmitted by many independent chains. Here, sensory certainty requires multiple consistent inputs.
This analogy helps connect to Islamic sciences.

أهمية الحواس

تحتل الحواس - بوصفها مصدراً للمعرفة وطريقة لها - أهمية لا يقلل من قدرها باحث منصف. فالحس أحد منافذ المعرفة إلى نفس الإنسان، ويكاد المرء العادي لا يعترف بسواها وسيلة لمعرفته إذ إن معارفه كلها مبنية على ما رآه وسمعه ولمسه بنفسه.
ومعظم حقائقه (Truths) عن الدنيا الموضوعية والدنيا الخارجية المحيطة به إنما هي مدركات حسية (۱).
ولا شك أن المعرفة الحسية تكون يقينية عندما يُقدم الحس شهادته مؤكدة قاطعة بعد عمليات الإدراك الحسي المتواترة، والمتفقة مع شهادات الحواس الأخرى، وشهادات الآخرين من غير أن تتعارض مع أصل من أصول العقل وقوانينه (۲)، مع أن بعض الباحثين لا يسلّم حتى بقانون العلية أو قوانين العقل الأولية تلك، إذ إن العلاقة بين العلة والمعلول لا تقوم -حسب هذه الوجهة- على مبدأ عدم التناقض الصوري، الذي يذهب إليه العقليـون بوصفه قانوناً حتمياً من قوانين العقل الأولية، وإنما على أساس التجربة (۳).
ومن قبل تزعم (جون لوك) لواء هذه المقولة في كتابه (مقالة في العقل البشري فذهب إلى إنكار وجود أفكار أولية موروثة، أو مايسمى ببدائــه العقول، كما يقول أصحاب المذهب العقلي.
ويرد (لوك) على ما يُقدّم من أدلة تؤكد صحة دعوتهم بما يلي: أولاً : إن القول بأن هذه الأفكار مسلم بها مـن جميـع النـاس بـلا استثناء
محجوج بوجود قبائل متوحشة لا تعرفها.
ثانياً : إن القول بأن العقل البشري يدرك هذه الأفكار بمجرد وعيه ويقظته محجوج بأن العقل لا يستطيع إدراك تلك الآراء بمجرد يقظته في السنين الأولى من العمر، بدليل أن الأطفال تجهلها تماماً. فالقول مثلاً باستحالة اجتماع النقيضين في وقت واحد أمر يجهله الأطفال والمتوحشون والبلهاء على الرغم من بداهته. وبذلك فلا يمكن القول بفطرية مثل هذه الآراء، بل هي مكتسبة بتجربة طويلة (١).
وعلى الرغم من حدّة الخلاف بين الحسيين والعقليين إلا أن ذلك لا يعني إنكار العقليين المطلق لأهمية الحس أو العكس، وإنمـا مـدار الخلاف كامن في أيهما الأصل، وأيهما الفرع له (٢).

(انظر: الإنسان والعقل لنايف معروف ۱۲ - ۱۳). وبالمقابل فإن الترانستتدالية تغالي في أهمية العقل إلى الحد الذي تجعله مصدراً أوحد للمعرفة، لأن العلوم كلها كامنة فيه، وعالم الحس يعمل على تشويش معارف العقل. (انظر: المرجع السابق للزنيدي، (٥٢٧ - ٥٢٨)
Chapter Three: Section One - Sensation
Importance of the Senses

The senses - as a source and method of knowledge - occupy an importance that no fair researcher diminishes. For sensation is one of the entrances of knowledge into the human soul, and the ordinary person almost does not recognize any other means for his knowledge, since all his knowledge is built on what he saw, heard, and touched himself. Most of his truths about the objective world and the external world surrounding him are sensory perceptions (1). There is no doubt that sensory knowledge becomes certain when the sense presents its testimony in a decisive and conclusive manner after repeated sensory perception processes, in agreement with the testimonies of other senses and the testimonies of others, without conflicting with any principle of the principles of reason and its laws (2), even though some researchers do not even accept the law of causality or those primary laws of reason, since the relationship between cause and effect - according to this view - is not based on the principle of non-contradiction, which the rationalists consider a necessary law of the primary laws of reason, but rather on experience (3). Previously, John Locke championed this view in his book "An Essay Concerning Human Understanding", denying the existence of innate ideas, or what is called the first principles of minds, as the rationalists say. Locke refutes the evidence presented to confirm the validity of their claim as follows: First: The claim that these ideas are accepted by all people without exception is refuted by the existence of savage tribes that do not know them. Second: The claim that the human mind perceives these ideas simply upon its awareness and wakefulness is refuted by the fact that the mind cannot perceive those ideas upon its wakefulness in the early years of life, as evidenced by the fact that children are completely ignorant of them. For example, the statement that it is impossible for two contradictories to coexist at the same time is something that children, savages, and the feebleminded are ignorant of, despite its self-evidence. Thus, it cannot be said that such ideas are innate, but rather they are acquired through long experience (1). Despite the intensity of the disagreement between empiricists and rationalists, that does not mean an absolute denial by rationalists of the importance of sensation or vice versa. Rather, the point of contention lies in which of them is the origin and which is the derivative (2). And if anything contrary to this occurs, it is a departure from the general direction of this or that side, and a reprehensible extremism whose consequences are not borne by the majority of empiricists or rationalists (3). With regard to the stance of the Noble Quran on experience or sensation, experience - in the view of some scholars of its verses - represents knowledge of the consequences of things, and this method in
(1) Adnan Zarzur, Maqala fi al-Ma'rifa, pp. 29-30.
(2) See regarding the position of the empiricists: Rajih al-Kurdi, previous reference, pp. 540-581; and al-Zanidi, previous reference, pp. 526-528. And regarding the position of the rationalists: al-Kurdi, previous reference, pp. 583-584; and al-Zanidi, previous reference, pp. 526-528; and Muhammad Jawad Rida, Falsafat al-Tarbiya, pp. 333-334. (3) This refers to the sect of al-Sumaniyya, who are (idol worshipers and believers in reincarnation; they were called al-Sumaniyya after Somnath in India. See: Nazariyyat al-Ma'rifa fi al-Quran by Ayatullah Jawad Amuli, footnote (1)). This sect claims that there is no way to know anything except through the five senses, they invalidated theoretical sciences, and they claimed that all doctrines are false. Their statement necessitates the invalidation of their own doctrine, because the statement invalidating a doctrine is itself a doctrine. (See: Usul al-Din by Abu Mansur al-Baghdadi, pp. 10-11; and also al-Farq bayn al-Firaq, p. 311). The sophists followed this doctrine. (See: al-Insan wa al-'Aql by Na'if Ma'ruf, pp. 12-13). Conversely, transcendentalism exaggerates the importance of reason to the extent that it makes it the sole source of knowledge, because all sciences are latent in it, and the world of sensation works to confuse the knowledge of reason. (See: previous reference of al-Zanidi, pp. 527-528)
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the role of sense perception in knowledge. The author begins by affirming the importance of the senses as a source of knowledge, noting that ordinary people rely almost exclusively on sensory experience for their understanding of the world. Sensory perceptions form the basis of most truths about the objective external world. The author then outlines the conditions under which sensory knowledge becomes certain: repeated sensory perceptions that are consistent across different senses and with the testimonies of others, and that do not contradict the fundamental principles of reason. However, the author notes that some researchers reject even the law of causality, which rationalists consider a primary rational principle, instead grounding causality in experience – an empiricist view. The author then presents John Locke's denial of innate ideas, as expressed in his 'Essay Concerning Human Understanding'. Locke argues against the rationalist position that certain ideas are innate and universally accepted. His first argument is that if these ideas were universally accepted, then all peoples would hold them, but there exist savage tribes who do not. His second argument is that if these ideas were perceived immediately upon mental awareness, then children would know them from infancy, but they are ignorant even of self-evident principles like the impossibility of contradiction. Therefore, such ideas are acquired through long experience. The author then clarifies that the debate between empiricists and rationalists is not about denying the other's contribution entirely, but about which is primary and which secondary. Extremism in either direction is condemned. Finally, the author briefly touches on the Quranic stance, indicating that experience (or sensation) is seen by some exegetes as providing knowledge of the consequences of things. This passage is an expansion on the epistemological debate, drawing on Western philosophical arguments but contextualizing them within an Islamic framework, as seen in the references to the Quran and Islamic sects like al-Sumaniyya. The author uses the example of the impossibility of contradiction (a principle of logic) to illustrate Locke's point, which is a good Islamic example as it relates to the law of non-contradiction used in Islamic logic (Kalam). The passage also warns against extreme positions like sensualism (al-Sumaniyya) and transcendentalism, both of which are criticized for their one-sidedness.

According to the passage, what condition is necessary for sensory knowledge to become certain?
A) It must be based on innate ideas.
B) It must be consistent with the law of non-contradiction and other rational principles.
C) It must be acquired through a single, vivid sense perception.
D) It must be confirmed by rationalist philosophers.

Key Takeaway
Sensory knowledge becomes certain only when it is repeated, consistent, and does not conflict with rational principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the three conditions from the passage, evaluate whether a claim such as 'I saw a mirage in the desert' can be considered certain sensory knowledge. Identify which conditions fail.
?
Find an example from the life of the Prophet (hadith) where multiple companions reported the same sensory event (e.g., the splitting of the moon). Explain how the conditions of repetition and consistency apply to verify that report.
